# FORMATION OF OBSERVATION EQUATIONS

In the first column of the following table is given the assumed azimuths of the various lines. Under station Gunner the azimuth to Duck, $2 9 9 ^ { \circ } \ : \ : 3 8 ^ { \prime } \ : \ : 0 4 ^ { \prime \prime } . 3$ ，comes from the position computation on page 110,and the other azimuths are obtained by adding to this the corrected angles from the preliminary computation of triangles. Thus in the first triangle in that list,Gunner-Duck-Larrabee, the angle at Gunner is $4 5 ^ { \circ } \ 4 3 ^ { \prime } \ 3 4 . ^ { \prime \prime } 2$ ，which,added to the azimuth of Duck, gives $3 4 5 ^ { \circ } \ 2 1 ^ { \prime } \ 3 8 ^ { \prime \prime } . 5$ ，as shown in the table. For any one station the assumed and observed azimuths of some one station may be taken as identical. At Gunner they are identical on station Duck. The observed azimuths in the second column of the table have their symbolic corrections affixed. These azimuths are obtained by adding the observed angles as derived from the list of observed directions on page 106 to the azimuth of the line Gunner-Duck.

At the fixed stations given in the lower part of the table the method of computing the assumed and observed azimuths is somewhat different. The assumed azimuths of the fixed lines come from the table of fixed positions on page 106. The assumed azimuths of the new lines are found by adding to one of these fixed azimuths the appropriate corrected angle from the computation of triangles on pages 107-109. In the second column of the table the observed azimuth of one fixed line used as an initial line is taken identical with its assumed azimuth，and the other observed azimuths,whether of fixed lines or of new ones,are found by adding to this azimuth the observed angles between the initial line and each of the others as derived from the list of directions.

The coefficients of the δ\$'s and sx's are found from the formulas on page 93.

Figure 6 taken with the following two tables shows that $\mathfrak { z } _ { 1 }$ is for directions taken at Gunner, $z _ { 2 }$ for directions at Cranberry Point, $z _ { 3 }$ for directions at Telegraph, and $z _ { 5 }$ for directions at Mam. The scheme for eliminating these z’s by the use of the sum equations, as fictitious additional observation equations with negative weights, is used here in the same manner as in the previous example. Each weight is the negative reciprocal of the total number of observed lines in the adjustment that radiates from the point in question. The weights re,rpeciely $- 1 / 8 , \ - 1 / 6 , \ - 1 / 4$ and $- 1 / 6$ ，as shown in Table 1.

At $\mathrm { T r e a t } _ { 2 } .$ ，Larrabee, Indian, and Duck where only one new line is to be determined the same process might be used,but the following method is identical in results and slightly shorter. Use is made of the fact that the directions taken at a point may each be changed by the same amount,a change equivalent to using merely a different zero point. Correct each of the directions by the averages of all the corrections necessary to reduce the observed results to the accepted results on the lines that have already been fixcd. Then drop the $_ { \mathcal { E } }$ from the observation equation of the new line and assign the equation a positive weight equal to $\frac { s } { s + 1 }$ ，where $s$ is the number of lines already fixed and therefore $s + 1$ is the total number of lines. Thus at Larrabee the constant terms of the observation equations representing pointings on lines already fixed are $+ 2 . 6 , \ : - 0 . 4 , \ : + 9 . 0 , \ : - 0 . 1 ,$ and 0.o, the mean of which is $+ 2 . 8$ . Subtracting this from each of the preceding numbers we have $- 0 . 2 , - 3 . 2 , + 6 . 2 , - 0 . 1$ and $- 2 . 8$ as the new constant terms, also $- 0 . 8$ instead of $+ 2 . 0$ on Gunner, the new point. These new values are inclosed in parentheses and are used in forming the normal equations. There are five fixed lines, so the weight of the new equation without $z$ that is used to replace the six equations containing $_ { z }$ is $5 / 6$ and the equation itself is

$$
v _ { 2 2 } = + 5 4 1 \hat { o } \phi _ { 1 } - 1 4 7 3 \hat { o } \lambda _ { 1 } - 0 . 8
$$

which in Table 2 corresponds to the line No. 22，

$$
+ 0 . 4 9 \delta \phi _ { 1 } - 1 . 3 4 \delta \lambda _ { 1 } - 0 . 0 7 .
$$

The $z ^ { \prime }$ s are computed from the sum equations as in the previous example, the result of substitution in the right-hand side being divided by $- 1 / r$ ， $\mathbfit { r }$ being the total number of lines through the point to which $_ { \mathcal { A } }$ applies. For fixed points where only one new line occurs, substitute $\delta \phi$ and $\delta \lambda$ in the right-hand side of the observation equation on the new line omitting the $z$ ,and divide the result by $- 1 / r$ .Thus at Duck (see p. 116),

$$
\mathcal { z } _ { 8 } = ( 8 6 6 9 \partial \phi _ { 1 } - 3 5 0 9 \partial \lambda _ { 1 } - 1 . 9 ) \div ( - \ 3 )
$$

as shown in the computation below.

When the z's are known the v's or corrections are computed from the equations on pages 115 and 116. The details are shown in the table on page 121.

For convenience of solution in the normals it is best to divide the constant terms by 10 and the coefficients by 100o. The solution will then give $1 0 0 \delta \phi _ { \mathbf { a } }$ and $1 0 0 \delta \lambda _ { \mathrm { { n } } }$

SECOND METHOD.

The only difference between the second method of adjustment and the first is in the treatment of the directions taken at the fixed points. At these points observation equations are written for the directions of new points only and the $z ^ { \prime }$ s are omitted. The observations taken over the fixed lines are not used, but the observed directions of the new lines are taken in connection with the adjusted direction of a fixed line,all directions being referred to a common initial line.

The equations with z's omitted are tho same as if the angle method of adjustment were used. (See p.196.) In this treatment these equations are given unit weight. Jordan (Vermessungskunde, vol.1,p. 179,of the third edition) suggests that on some accounts it would be beter to assign the equations for observations at fixed points only half weight.

The observation equations for directions taken at Gunner, Cranberry Point, and Telegraph are the samo as for the frst method given on page 115 and are not repeated here. Below are given the observation cquations for the remaining points,formed according to the second method. The assumed azimuths are identical with those used in the first method. As an example, to illustrate the computation of the observed azimuths, take the line Mam-Gunner. Use the observed direction for the new line and the adjusted one for the fixed line..

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { \mathrm { F i x e d ~ a z i m u t h ~ M a m ~ t o ~ I n d i a n ~ P o i n t , p a g e ~ 1 0 6 , = 2 6 6 } } & { 1 7 \quad 1 9 . 2 } \\ & { \mathrm { A n g l e ~ I n d i a n ~ P o i n t ~ t o ~ G u n n e r , p a g e ~ 1 0 6 ~ ( 3 5 9 ^ { \circ } } } & \\ & { \mathrm { 5 9 ^ { \prime } ~ 5 5 ^ { \prime \prime } . 4 ~ t o ~ 3 2 2 ^ { \circ } ~ 0 1 ^ { \prime } ~ 4 4 ^ { \prime \prime } . 8 ) , } } & { \mathrm { = } } & { \mathrm { = 3 2 2 } \quad 0 1 \quad 4 9 . 4 } \\ & { \mathrm { O b s e r v e d ~ a z i m u t h ~ M a m ~ t o ~ G u n n e r } , } & { \mathrm { = 2 2 8 ~ 1 9 \quad 0 8 . ~ 6 8 . } } \end{array}
$$

or by reckoning from any other fixed line through Mam the same result isd

Fixed azimuth Mam to Lubec Channel Light- · / house, ${ \bf \ = } 2 2 5$ 14 19.0 Angle Lubec Channel Lighthouse to Gunner, page106 $3 1 8 ^ { \circ } 5 6 ^ { \prime } 5 5 ^ { \prime \prime } . 2$ to 322°01'44".8), = 3 04 49.6 Observed azimuth Mam to Gunner, =228 19 08.6

Note that tho coefficients of the $\delta \phi$ s and ox's are exactly the same as for the first method and that the $z ^ { \prime }$ sare omitted.

The first part of each of tables 1 and 2 for the formation of normals according to the first method,pages 118and 119,down tothe line for $v _ { 1 9 }$ will serve for the second method also and is not repeated here. The remainder of the tables according to the second method is given below.

In forming the normal equations the four z's that occur are eliminated by the device of the sum equation serving as a fictitious observation equation with negative weight. The other observations that do not contain z’s enter into the formation of the normal equations in the usual way. After the normal equations have been solved the four z's are found from the sum equations in the way previously explained and enter into the computation of the v's, or corrections, from 1 to 18 and 20 and 21,but not into the others.

Computation of probable errors   

<table><tr><td rowspan=1 colspan=1></td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1>Adoptedfirstsolution</td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1>U2</td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1></td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1>Adoptedv&#x27;ssecondsolution</td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1>&amp;2</td></tr><tr><td rowspan=3 colspan=1>中</td><td rowspan=3 colspan=1>+111+++1++1++++|1++1++111++11+11+111++11 2579195571160938848761855267776392587504</td><td rowspan=2 colspan=1>44252062626861138004294364936442525476499493861426492530561</td><td rowspan=3 colspan=1>12３456789101121314151671819202122324</td><td rowspan=3 colspan=1>1111+++1++1++++丨1.+++++++ </td><td rowspan=1 colspan=1></td></tr><tr><td rowspan=2 colspan=1>734.93</td></tr><tr><td rowspan=1 colspan=1>895.72</td></tr></table>

In the first method of adjustment, there are 40 equations to determine 14 unknown quantities, namely, $6 \delta \phi$ s and ox's and. $\mathbf { \boldsymbol { 8 } } \boldsymbol { z } ^ { \prime }$ s. The probable error of an observed direction is therefore,

$$
0 . 6 7 4 5 { \sqrt { \frac { 8 9 5 . 7 2 } { 4 0 - 1 4 } } } { = } \pm 4 . 0
$$

In the second method there are 24 equations to determine 9 unknown quantities,mely $6 \delta \phi ^ { ; }$ s and $\delta \lambda ^ { : }$ sand $_ { 3 z }$ s at new points. The probable error of an observed direction is therefore,

$$
0 . 6 7 4 5 { \sqrt { \frac { 7 3 4 . 9 3 } { 1 5 } } } { = } \pm 4 . 7
$$

ADJUSTMENT OF A FIGURE CONTAINING LATITUDE, LONGITUDE, AZIMUTH,AND LENGTH CONDITIONS BY THE METHOD OF VARIA-TION OF GEOGRAPHIC COORDINATES

This example illustrates the ftting of a chain of triangulation in between fixed lines at the ends. The necessary preliminary computations of the assumed positions and directions could have been carried out in much the same way as in the preceding examples. A preliminary figure adjustment was, however, available and the results of it were used in the preliminary computations of the triangles and the geographic positions, pages 140-157. The fixed lines at the ends of the chain are shown in the following list of fixed positions. The list of observed directions is not given. The necessary data may be derived from the observed angles of the triangles, pages 140-143,taken in connection with figure 7, page 157.

# FORMATION OF OBSERVATION EQUATIONS

The position computation was carried westward from the fixed lines at the eastern end of the schemc and the observation equations were formcd in the same order. The treatment of fixcd lincs is the same as in the first adjustment of figure 6, pagcs 105 ct scq. No ncw detail arises until the points Deer and Ship are reached, which have lines connccting them with the fixcd points Biloxi Lighthouse and Ship Island Lighthouse. Suppose for the moment that Biloxi Lighthouse were not fixcd but that its latitude and longitude were to reccive corrections of $\delta \phi _ { 1 2 }$ and $\delta \lambda _ { 1 2 }$ respectively. The observation equation for $v _ { 5 7 }$ would then rcad,

$$
v _ { 5 7 } = \tilde { e } _ { 1 3 } - 6 0 3 \partial \tilde { \phi } _ { 1 1 } + 2 4 8 \tilde { \partial } \lambda _ { 1 1 } + 6 0 3 \partial \tilde { \phi } _ { 1 2 } - 2 4 8 \tilde { \partial } \lambda _ { 1 2 } + 0 . 5
$$

The latitude and longitude of Biloxi Lighthouse as developed by the preliminary position computation are $3 0 ^ { \circ } \ \mathrm { 2 3 ^ { \prime } \ 3 9 . ^ { \prime \prime } 4 1 9 }$ and $8 8 ^ { \circ }$ $5 4 ^ { \prime } ~ 0 3 . ^ { \prime \prime } 9 5 4 ~ $ ,while the fixed values are $3 0 ^ { \circ } \ 2 3 ^ { \prime } \ 3 9 . ^ { \prime \prime } 4 1 9$ and $8 8 ^ { \circ } \ 5 4 ^ { \prime }$ $0 3 . { } ^ { \prime \prime } 8 2 0 $ ， so that to rcduce the preliminary to the fixcd valucs on which the adjustmcnt is built corrections of $\partial \phi _ { 1 2 } = 0 . 0 0 0 ^ { \ast }$ and $\partial \lambda _ { 1 2 } = ~ - 0 . 1 3 4$ are necessary. By substituting thcse valucs in the equation for $v _ { 5 7 }$ there results,

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { v _ { 5 7 } = z _ { 1 3 } - 6 0 3 \partial \phi _ { 1 1 } + 2 4 8 \partial \lambda _ { 1 1 } + 6 0 3 ~ ( 0 . 0 0 0 ) - 2 4 8 ~ ( - 0 . 1 3 4 ) + 0 . 5 } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { v _ { 5 7 } = z _ { 1 3 } - 6 0 3 \partial \phi _ { 1 1 } + 2 4 8 \partial \lambda _ { 1 1 } + 3 3 . 7 } } \end{array}
$$

as given in the table page 16.

The equation for the reverse dircction, $v _ { \scriptscriptstyle { \mathrm { 6 3 } } } ,$ if Biloxi Lighthouse were not fixed, would be,

$$
v _ { 6 3 } = \tilde { z } _ { 1 5 } - 6 0 3 \partial \hat { \phi } _ { 1 1 } + 2 4 8 \hat { \sigma } \lambda _ { 1 1 } + 6 0 3 \partial \hat { \phi } _ { 1 2 } - 2 4 8 \hat { \sigma } \lambda _ { 1 2 } + 0 . 0
$$

which with the use of the above values of $\delta \phi _ { 1 2 }$ and $\delta \lambda _ { 1 2 }$ becomes,

$$
v _ { { 6 3 } } = z _ { 1 5 } - 6 0 3 \partial \phi _ { 1 1 } + 2 4 8 \partial \lambda _ { 1 1 } + 3 3 . 2
$$

Similar computations must bc made for $v _ { { 5 6 } } , \ v _ { { 5 8 } } , \ v _ { { 5 9 } } , \ v _ { { 6 4 } } , \ v _ { { 6 7 } } ,$ and $v _ { \mathrm { { 6 8 } } }$ ：

The known terms in the exprcssions for $v _ { \mathrm { { 6 5 } } }$ ,and ${ \boldsymbol { v } } _ { \mathrm { 6 6 } }$ may be found in a similar way by allowing for the fixity of both ends of the linc. If the corrections necded to the prcliminary position of Ship Island Lighthouse in order to reduceit to the fixed position are called $\delta \phi _ { 1 3 }$ and $\delta { \lambda } _ { 1 3 }$ then for the line Biloxi Lighthouse to Ship Island Lighthouse the formula gives,

$$
d \alpha = 8 9 ( \partial \phi _ { 1 3 } - \partial \phi _ { 1 2 } ) + 2 5 0 ( \partial \lambda _ { 1 3 } - \partial \lambda _ { 1 2 } )
$$

If the line were free to bc turned in azimuth, thcn by thc adjustment,

$$
v _ { 6 5 } = 2 z _ { 1 5 } + 8 9 ( \partial \phi _ { 1 3 } - \partial \phi _ { 1 2 } ) + 2 5 0 ( \partial \lambda _ { 1 3 } - \partial \lambda _ { 1 2 } ) - 1 . 7
$$

But to reduce the positions of the preliminary computation to the fixed positions, $\delta \phi _ { 1 3 } = - 0 . 0 0 2$ ， $\partial \phi _ { 1 2 } = 0 . 0 0 0$ ， $\partial \lambda _ { 1 3 } = - 0 . 0 8 0$ ，and $\partial \lambda _ { 1 2 } = - 0 . 1 3 4$ (see pp.55,157). ustituting tse uesi

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } { . \ } & { { } } & { \mathcal { V } _ { \mathrm { e 5 } } = z _ { 1 5 } + 1 1 . 6 } \end{array}
$$

as given in the table. $v _ { \scriptscriptstyle { \mathrm { B } } \mathrm { 6 } }$ is found by a similar process.

The effect of using for the preliminary computation values from a previous adjustment for the figure but not for the positions appears in the constant term of the normal equations until the effect of closure in positions comes in. These constant terms should be zero except for the effect of accumulated errors in the last place of the two computations and a slight difference in the treatment of directions (3) and (3a). They are in fact almost negligible until the effect of closure appears in the cquation for $\delta \phi _ { 8 }$ ,from which point they become quite large.

# ADJUSTMENTS BY THE ANGLE METHOD

If the adjustment be made according to the angle method\* the complications due to the presence of the z's are avoided. An angle is the difference of two directions and the observation equation for an observed angle is the difference of the observation equations of its two sides，and in taking the diference the $z$ drops out. " To illustrate this suppose thatatstation Gunner,Figure 6,page 4,the folowing angles were observed:Duck to Indian Point, Indian Point to Larrabee, Larrabee to Mam， Mam to Lubec Channel Lighthouse, and Lubec Channel Lighthouse to Lubec Church spire. Call the corrections to the observed angles $u _ { 1 } , u _ { 2 } , u _ { 3 } , u _ { 4 } ,$ and $u _ { 5 }$ respectively,and suppose the observed and assumed values to be as given on page 115. Then

$$
\begin{array} { l } { { u _ { 1 } { = } v _ { 2 } { - } v _ { 1 } { = } - 6 1 3 1 \hat { \sigma } \phi _ { 1 } { + } 1 8 0 0 \hat { \sigma } \lambda _ { 1 } { - } 2 . 2 } } \\ { { \nonumber } } \\ { { u _ { 2 } { = } v _ { 3 } { - } v _ { 2 } { = } - 1 9 9 7 \hat { \sigma } \phi _ { 1 } { + } 2 3 6 \hat { \sigma } \lambda _ { 1 } { + } 7 . 0 } } \end{array}
$$

In a similar way,

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { u _ { 3 } = - 2 7 3 2 \partial \phi _ { 1 } + \phantom { - } 8 5 \partial \lambda _ { 1 } - 1 . 2 } \\ & { u _ { 4 } = - 5 5 6 5 \partial \phi _ { 1 } - 2 4 4 5 \partial \lambda _ { 1 } + 3 . 5 } \\ & { u _ { 5 } = + 5 8 8 6 \partial \phi _ { 1 } + 5 4 1 3 \partial \lambda _ { 1 } + 1 8 . 4 } \end{array}
$$

These contain no $z ^ { \prime }$ s and the normal equations. may be formed in the usual way.

Observation equations of this kind would arise when at an unknown point angles are taken on known points,as for example when angles are taken with a sextant from a point off-shore to determine its position，and for such observations the angle method is both easier and more logical than the direction method.

# ADJUSTMENT OF VERTICAL OBSERVATIONS

GENERAL STATEMENT

When reciprocal vertical observations are made over the lines of a triangulation scheme a computation of the differences of elevations is

made by the usual Coast and Geodetic Survey formula. For an account of these observations and of the method of computation,see United States Coast and Geodetic Survey Special Publication No. 19, page 140 et seq. As thcre are always several lines from each station, rigid conditions are present in the figure. Thus it becomes necessary to make an adjustment of the observed values by the method of least squares. In the following figure the differences of elevations as observed are first computed and then the results are adjusted by the method employed in the United States Coast and Geodetic Survcy.

The formula used in the following computations is the one given in Special Publication No.19,mentioned above. On pages 205 et seq. there is given a new development of the formula that takes into account some of the small terms that

![](images/16c59c8f1a9e2227b0c8f1b308efe09e7f14f72701dd11b3631fd2b6b859dbbf.jpg)

are needed in computation over longer and higher lines. The final form of the new formula differs slightly from the one used in this computation.

Computation of elevations from reciprocal observations

The adjustment of vertical observations as practiced in the United States Coast and Geodetic Survey is made by means of observation equations and differs somewhat from the method of conditions. Of course condition equations couldbeemployed if it weredesred,just as triangulation can be adjusted by observation equations. (Se the adjustmentbythe Variation of GeographicCoordinates，p.91eteq.)

Elevations for the various stations are assumed somewhat near what the final values will be. To these are added $x '$ s to be determined by the adjustment. (See table of assumed elevations on p. 200.) By means of these,observation equations are formed by the comparison of the assumed $h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 }$ with that determined by computation.

The method of formation is shown below and a tabulated form of all of the computation on page 201.

# FORMATION OF OBSERVATION EQUATIONS

The observation equations are formed as follows:

(1) Pollywog, assumed elevation\*= $8 1 1 + x _ { 1 }$

FORMATION OF NORMAL EQUATIONS BY DIFFERENTIATION

The following equations are formed from the table just given:

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { - v _ { 1 } = - 1 . 9 3 + x _ { 1 } } \\ & { - v _ { 2 } = - 0 . 8 3 + x _ { 1 } } \\ & { - v _ { 3 } = + 1 . 9 2 + x _ { 1 } } \\ & { - v _ { 4 } = + 0 . 7 2 + x _ { 2 } } \\ & { - v _ { 5 } = - 0 . 2 5 - x _ { 1 } + x _ { 4 } } \end{array}
$$

and so on for the rest of the 27 v's.

The function $^ { u }$ to be made a minimum is ${ { \dot { 2 } } p _ { n } } { v _ { n } } ^ { 2 }$ ，or $u = + 1 . 3 2 ( - 1 . 9 3 + x _ { 1 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 3 4 ( - 0 . 8 3 + x _ { 1 } ) ^ { 2 } + 1 . 5 1 ( + 1 . 9 2 + x _ { 1 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 7 2 ( + 0 . 7 2 + x _ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 }$ $+ 2 . 6 0 ( - 0 . 2 5 - x _ { 1 } + x _ { 2 } ) ^ { 2 } + 1 0 . 8 9 ( - 0 . 7 3 - x _ { 2 } + x _ { 3 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 4 7 ( + 6 . 9 0 + x _ { 3 } ) ^ { 2 } + 2 . 8 2 ( + 3 . 9 0 + x _ { 4 } ) ^ { 2 } + 2 . 1 1 ( - 0 . 0 0 + x _ { 5 } ) ^ { 2 }$ ） $- x _ { 1 } + x _ { 3 } ) ^ { 2 } + 4 . 5 4 ( - 3 . 5 5 - x _ { 2 } + x _ { 4 } ) ^ { 2 } + 4 . 8 9 ( + 2 . 1 6 - x _ { 3 } + x _ { 4 } ) ^ { 2 } + 8 . 5 3 ( + 1 . 6 1 - x _ { 4 } + x _ { 5 } ) ^ { 2 } + 4 . 5 3 ( - 3 . 5 5 + x _ { 6 } + x _ { 7 } ) ^ { 2 } + 4 . 5 3 ( - 3 . 5 5 + x _ { 1 } + x _ { 1 } ) ^ { 2 } )$ $\vdash 2 . 3 4 ( - 0 . 1 2 - x _ { 2 } + x _ { 5 } ) ^ { 2 } + 1 . 6 3 ( + 4 . 0 2 - x _ { 3 } + x _ { 5 } )$ 2+3.71（+2.90-x4+𝑥g）²+2.63（-0.08 $- x _ { 5 } + x _ { 6 } ) ^ { z } + 4 . 9 3 ( - 1 . 5 8 - x _ { 6 } + x _ { 7 } ) ^ { z } + 2 . 6 1 ( + 1 . 8 3 - x _ { 4 } + x _ { 7 } ) ^ { z } + 5 . 1 9 ( + 0 . 3 1 - x _ { 5 } + x _ { 7 } ) ^ { z }$ $+ 0 . 3 6 ( - 0 . 6 7 + x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { z } + 0 . 4 2 ( + 2 . 7 3 - x _ { 6 } + x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { z } + 1 . 0 6 ( - \mathrm { L } . 0 1 - x _ { 7 } + x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { z } + 3 . 4 3$ （ $- 0 . 2 2 - x _ { 7 } + x _ { 8 } ) ^ { 2 } + 2 . 0 3 ( + 0 . 6 4 + x _ { 8 } - x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 2 4 ( - 0 . 1 1 + x _ { 9 } ) ^ { 2 } + 8 . 3 8 ( - 0 . 3 0 - x _ { 8 } + x _ { 9 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 2 4 ( - 0 . 1 1 + x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { 2 } + 0 . 2 4 ( - 0 . 1 1 + x _ { 9 } ) ^ { 2 }$ $+ 2 . 3 7 ( + 0 . 2 7 - x _ { 7 } + x _ { 9 } ) ^ { 2 } + 7 . 3 8 ( + 0 . 1 8 + x _ { 9 } - x _ { 1 0 } ) ^ { 2 } .$

$$

(See the table of normals on p. 204.)

Tho normals are most conveniently formed from the table given on page 204. The various observation equations are written along the horizontal lines in the columns of their respective x’s. The normals are then formed as in condition equations, except that the constant terms must also be multiplied by each column and the sums taken for the constant terms in the normals, as may be seon from the direct computation of the normals abovo.

After the x's are determined from the solution of the normals, they are added to the assumed elevations, giving the adjusted final elevations. The v's are most easily determincd by computing $h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 }$ from the adjusted values; if the observed $h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 }$ is subtracted from the adjusted value the respective $v$ results. They could, of course, be computed by substituting the $x ^ { \prime }$ s in the observation equations, but this would require more work.

For a check the $\boldsymbol { \Sigma }$ pv at any station should equal zero, with the possible exception of a small amount due to dropping the decimals on the $x ^ { \prime } \mathrm { s }$ 。 In the table on pago 201, use $p v$ from the first column if the $_ x$ is positive and from the second column if the $x$ is negative.

DEVELOPMENT OF FORMULAS FOR TRIGONOMETRIC LEVELING

# GENERAL STATEMENT

The formulas used on pagos 198 and 199in the computation of vertical observations were found to be lacking in some of tho quantities that were appreciablo when tho lines were verylong and high. Accordingly，a new derivation is now given that takes into account some of these quantities. As a result, tho formulas derived in this development differ slightly from those used in the computation cited above, but they ought to give practically the same rosult in computing over lines of such length as occur therein.

The following derivation of the formulas for trigonometric leveling is based on certain approximate assumptions which fall under four general heads:

1. Geometric approximations.-The verticals at the two points $( \mathcal { P } _ { 1 }$ the point occupied and $P _ { 2 }$ the point sighted on) are treated as if they lay in one plane and the intersection of this plane with the ellipsoid that represents the surface of the earth is treated as the arc of a circle whose radius is the mean radius of curvature of a vertical section through $P _ { 1 }$ and $\mathrm { { P _ { 2 } } }$ Helmert (in his Hohere Geodasie,Vol.I, p. 520,and Vol. II, p.563) investigates the error arising from these assumptions and finds it to be about $1 / 4 0$ meter at a maximum when the distance $P _ { 1 } \ : P _ { 2 }$ is about 100 kilometers.

2. Geodetic approximations.-The difference between the geodetic zenith and the astronomic zenith, i.e., the deflection of the plumb line,isigdIftseeecionareowrectios be applied tothe measured zithdistances (whichofcourse,re d to the astronomical zenith) to reduce them to the geodetic zenith. Furthermore, the elevations obtained by trigonometric leveling between two pntsare reerred toanassumed eipsoid,whileiit leveling gives elevations referred to the geoid,so that the distances between geoid and ellipsoid must be known to make the two kinds of leveling comparable. If trigonometric leveling could be carried out with great precision,its use in connection with spirit leveling would give just this information as to the distance of the ellipsoid from the geoid. The change in the distance from geoid to ellipsoid occurring between $P _ { 1 }$ and $P _ { 2 }$ may be found from the deflections of the vertical atthose pteditisdtio uniformly between $P _ { 1 }$ and $P _ { 2 } ,$ an assumption which may be considerably in error.

3. Optical approximations.-The path of the ray of light between $P _ { 1 }$ and $P _ { 2 }$ is assumed to be the arc of a circle in a vertical plane through $P _ { 1 }$ and $P _ { 2 }$ The angle between the chord $P _ { 1 }$ $P _ { 2 }$ and the tangent to the circle at either point is the refraction in zenith distance andit is evidently implied that this refraction is equal at $P _ { 1 }$ and $P _ { 2 }$ · If we call $o$ (see figure 9) the center of the circle referred to in approximation 1,and call the angle $P _ { 1 } O P _ { 2 } { = } \theta$ ,the refraction in zenith distance of the angle $T P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 }$ $\bf \Pi ^ { \prime } = \angle \it T P _ { 2 } P _ { 1 } )$ is written as mθ and $m$ is termed the coeffcient of refraction. The course of a ray of light through the atmosphere depends on the variations in pressure, temperature and humidity of the medium through which it passesand may be far from circular. Our lack of knowledge of the conditions which govern the refraction is the greatest obstacle to precision in trigonometric leveling.

4. Algebraic approximations.-After tho approximaticns mentioned above have been made, there is the further approximation arising from the dropping of small terms after an expansion in serics. In the following developments it will be seen that only extremely small terms are dropped,and that in cases arising in practice their effect even on the sixth placo of logarithms is unimportant, whilo in fact logarithms of only five places are commonly used for this sort of computation. .The accuracy of the developments is confirmed by the numerical agreement between the approximate and the exact formulas in tho examples given. (Exact is used in the sense of dispensing with the use of series. The formula is inexact, owing to the first threo sots of approximations.) The examples represent rather oxtrome cases of thoso arising in practico,and other numerical examples of oxtreme casos givo a similar agreement.

# DEVELOPMENT OF THE FORMULAS

\begin{array} { r l } { \angle V _ { 1 } P _ { 1 } T = \zeta _ { 1 } } & { { } } \\ { \mathrm { a n d ~ } \quad } & { { } \angle V _ { 2 } P _ { 2 } T = \zeta _ { 2 } . } \end{array}
$$

The measurements aro not made exactly in this plane, but tho error, which is prt of that involved in approximation 1,isglgible.

The refraction in zenithdistance is,according toapproximation3,

$$

With the tables here given $\textcircled { < } \textcircled { < }$ will probably be found slightly more convenient for logarithmic computation than $\cdot \mathcal { H }$ . The two forms are equally accurate.

# Case II. Only one zenith distance $\displaystyle \left( \zeta _ { 1 } \right)$ observed

Where two zenith distances are known,the formula,either (4)or (5),doesnotioetecoietofrcion $( m )$ explicitly. Where only one zenith distance is known, a value of. $m$ must be assumed from the best sources of information available.

In the triangle $P _ { 1 } \ L _ { 2 } \ P _ { 2 }$

$$
\angle P _ { 1 } \angle _ { 2 } P _ { 2 } = 9 0 ^ { \circ } + { \frac { \theta } { 2 } } = \angle V _ { 1 } P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 }
$$

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \angle P _ { 2 } P _ { 1 } L = \angle V _ { 1 } P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } - \angle V _ { 1 } P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 } } } \\ { { { } } } \\ { { = 9 0 ^ { \circ } + { \frac { \theta } { 2 } } - ( \zeta _ { 1 } + \angle \zeta ) = 9 0 ^ { \circ } - \zeta + ( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m ) \theta } } \end{array}
$$

For the third angle we find, by subtracting the sum of the other angles from $1 8 0 ^ { \circ }$

By the law of sines

$$
\angle P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 } L = \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 1 - m ) \theta .
$$

$$
\frac { L _ { 2 } P _ { 2 } } { P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } } { = } \frac { \sin P _ { 2 } P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } } { \sin P _ { 1 } P _ { 2 } L _ { 2 } }
$$

or

$$
h _ { 2 } { - } h _ { 1 } { = } P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } \frac { \cos \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } { - } \left( \frac { 1 } { 2 } { - } m \right) \theta \right] } { \sin \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } { - } \left( 1 { - } m \theta \right) \right] } .
$$

The chord $\scriptstyle P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } =$ chord $S _ { 1 } S _ { 2 } \times \frac { \rho + h _ { 1 } } { \rho } = \mathrm { c h o r }$ d $S _ { 1 } S _ { 2 } \times A$ ， $A$ having thoe meaning previously given; chord $S _ { 1 } S _ { 2 } = \operatorname { a r c }$ 8 very nearly; or, if greater precision is desired, $P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } = s A R$ ，where $R$ is the reduction factor from arc to chord.

The logarithm of the reduction factor from arc to sine is given in the Coast and Geodetic Survey Special Publication No.8 (Formule and Tables for the Computation of Geodetic Positions)， page 17. The logarithm of the reduction to chord is very nearly one-fourth of the reduction from are to sine. Granting approximations 1, 2，and 3, equation (6) may be rewritten as the so-called exact formula in the following form:

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s A R \frac { \cos { \left( \zeta _ { 1 } - ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } \right) } } { \sin { \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } - \frac { 1 } { 2 } \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } \right] } } .
$$

Sin ${ \bf 1 ^ { \prime \prime } }$ is introduced to convert the angle from radians to seconds of arc. $\mathcal { A }$ and $\mathcal { R }$ have the meanings previously indicated. The quantity $( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m )$ appears in the computation of the refraction from reciprocal zenith distances on the Coast and Geodetic Survey forms. A mean of the determinations of $\left( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m \right)$ from the reciprocal zenith distances should be used in computing the nonreciprocal observations.

Having found theangle $\zeta _ { 1 } - ( \frac { \ l _ { 1 } } { 2 } - m ) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 ^ { \prime \prime } } }$ )oinf puter should subtract $\frac { \delta } { ^ 2 } \ \frac { \delta } { \rho \sin { 1 ^ { \prime \prime } } }$ from it to get the angle for the denominator. The angle in tho denominator need not bo carried out very accurately， as it is always near $9 0 ^ { \circ }$ where the sine varies slowly.

The former Coast and Goodotic Survey forimula was

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + \frac { \left( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m \right) s ^ { 2 } } { \rho } + \frac { \left( 1 - m \right) s ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } } { \rho } .
$$

It is obtained from (6) or (7) by expanding in series and dropping certain small quantities. On some of the longer lines the quantities dropped are appreciable in computations with five-place logarithms. The development hereafter given will show that the general form of (8) may be retained by the introduction of corroction-factors $\mathcal { D } _ { \imath }$ and $D _ { 2 } ,$ which are nearly unityand by the further factor $\varLambda$ ,the correction-factor for elevation of the occupied station. The full formula will then be,

$$
h _ { 2 } { - h _ { 1 } } = A \ D _ { 1 } \ s \ \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + \frac { \left( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m } \right) A \ D _ { 2 } s ^ { 2 } } { \rho } + \frac { \left( 1 - m \right) s ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } } { \rho } ,
$$

This form may be obtained from (6) as follows: As before

$$
P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } = 2 ( \rho + h _ { 1 } ) \sin { \frac { \theta } { 2 } }
$$

or expanding by the sine series

$$
P _ { 1 } L _ { 2 } = \rho \left( 1 + \frac { \hbar _ { 1 } } { \rho } \right) \left[ \theta - \frac { \theta ^ { 3 } } { 2 4 } + \mathbf { \nabla } \cdot \mathbf { \nabla } \cdot \mathbf { \tilde { \mathbf { \pi } } } \right] = A _ { \mathcal { O } } \left( \theta - \frac { \theta ^ { 3 } } { 2 4 } + \mathbf { \nabla } \cdot \mathbf { \nabla } \cdot \mathbf { \tilde { \mathbf { \pi } } } \right) .
$$

The factor cos $[ \zeta _ { 1 } - \left( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m \right) ~ 0 ]$ in (6) may be written

cos $[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( \sharp - m ) \ 0 ] = \cos \ \zeta _ { 1 }$ cos $[ ( { \mit \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m ) \ 0 ] + \sin \ \zeta _ { 1 }$ sin $\left\lfloor \left( { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m \right) \right.$

Since $\left( { \scriptstyle { \frac { 1 } { 2 } } } - m \right)$ $\theta$ is a small quantity, the series forms for its sine and cosine may beusediing

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) \theta \right] = \cos \zeta _ { 1 } \bigg [ 1 - ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) ^ { 2 } \frac { \theta ^ { 2 } } { 2 } + \mathrm { ~  ~ \cdot ~ } \cdot \mathrm { ~  ~ \cdot ~ } \bigg ] } \\ { + \sin \zeta _ { 1 } \bigg [ ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) \theta - ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) ^ { 3 } \frac { \theta ^ { 3 } } { 6 } + \mathrm { ~  ~ \cdot ~ } \cdot \mathrm { ~  ~ \cdot ~ } \bigg ] } \end{array}
$$

The third factor on the right-hand side of (6), namely,

$$
 { \frac { 1 } { \sin \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 1 - m ) \theta \right] } } { = } \mathrm { c o s e c } \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 1 - m ) \theta \right]
$$

may be expanded in powers of $( 1 - m ) \theta$ by Taylor's theorem.

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \qquad f ( \zeta _ { 1 } ) = \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } } \\ & { \qquad f ^ { \prime } ( \zeta _ { 1 } ) = - \mathrm { c o t ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \mathrm { ~ c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } } \\ & { \qquad f ^ { \prime \prime } ( \zeta _ { 1 } ) = \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \mathrm { ~ ( 1 + 2 ~ c o t ^ 2 ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } ) } \\ & { \qquad f ^ { \prime \prime \prime } ( \zeta _ { 1 } ) = - 6 \mathrm { ~ c o s e c ^ { 3 } ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \mathrm { ~ c o t ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } + \mathrm { c o t ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \mathrm { ~ c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } . } \end{array}
$$

This gives,

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { \frac { 1 } { \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 1 - m ) \ O \right] } = \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } + \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \cot \zeta _ { 1 } ( 1 - m ) \ \theta } \\ & { } & { + \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { : } \left( 1 + 2 \ \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } \right) ( 1 - m ) ^ { 2 } \frac { \theta ^ { 2 } } { 2 } } \\ & { } & { + \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } \zeta _ { 1 } \cot \zeta _ { 1 } \left( 6 \ \mathrm { c o s e c ~ } ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } - 1 \right) ( 1 - m ) ^ { 3 } \frac { \theta ^ { 3 } } { 6 } + \cdot } \end{array}
$$

The expressions (9), (10),and (11) for the factors on the right-hand side of (6) are now to be multiplied together.

In cases that actually occur, $\boldsymbol { \theta }$ and cot $\zeta _ { 1 }$ are small quantities of about the same order of magnitude. If we call cot $\zeta _ { 1 }$ a quantity of the first order,it iseident that cosec $\zeta _ { 1 }$ differs from unity by a quantity of the second order. In forming the product from (9), (1o),and (11) it is seen that the product is of the second order,and will moreover contain only terms of even order, so that if terms of the fourth order are retained the error will be of the sixth order, or the proportional error (the error as compared with the quantity itself) will be of the fourth order or of tho order of f3otpartof the difference of elevai $\frac { 1 } { 3 0 }$ liberal allowance. The error, then,of the omitted terms should not affect the fifth place of logarithms and probably not the sixth. It will bo seen that tho expansions (9),(1o), and (11) have been carricd out sufficiently far for the purpose in hand,and if these oxpressions be multiplied togother, retaining in the product no terms of higher order than the fourth, the rosult may be written:

$$
\begin{array} { c }  { \displaystyle h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = \left( \rho + h _ { 1 } \right) \bigg \{ \theta \cot \zeta _ { 1 } \bigg [ 1 + \frac { 6 { \bf \Upsilon } ( 1 - m ) ^ { 2 } - 1 } { 6 } \cdot \mathrm {  ~ \} \theta ^ { 2 } \bigg ] } } \\ { { + { \bf \Upsilon } \left( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m \right) \theta ^ { 2 } \bigg [ 1 + \frac { 5 - 1 0 m + 4 { \bf \Upsilon } m ^ { 2 } } { 1 2 } \cdot \mathrm {  ~ \ } \theta ^ { 2 } \bigg ] + ( 1 - m ) \theta ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } \bigg \} } } \end{array}
$$

Since $\theta = { \frac { s } { \rho } } ,$ we may write

$$
\begin{array} { c } { { \displaystyle h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = A D _ { 1 } s \ \mathrm { c o t } \ \zeta _ { 1 } + \frac { A D _ { 2 } ( \frac 1 2 - m ) s ^ { 2 } } { \rho } + \frac { ( 1 - m ) s ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } } { \rho } \ } } \\ { { \mathrm { e r e } \ D _ { 1 } = 1 + \frac { 6 \ ( 1 - m ) ^ { 2 } - 1 } { 6 } \cdot \ \frac { s ^ { 2 } } { \rho ^ { 2 } } \ \mathrm { \ c } } } \\ { { \displaystyle D _ { 2 } = 1 + \frac { 5 - 1 0 \ m + 4 \ m ^ { 2 } } { 1 2 } \cdot \ \frac { s ^ { 2 } } { \rho ^ { 2 } } \ } } \end{array}
$$

The factor $\boldsymbol { A }$ has been omitted from the last term as being unnecessary， the latter being small and $\varLambda$ near unity. $D _ { 1 }$ and $\boldsymbol { D } _ { 2 }$ are also near unity. Their logarithms are tabulated in the same manner as the other quantities, the tables showing the limiting valucs of the argument between which log $\mathcal { D } _ { 1 }$ or log $\mathcal { D } _ { 2 }$ may be taken as 1, 2, 3, etc., units of the ffth decimal.

It may bo noted that in some European surveys the torm (1-m）8²cott \$1 is dropped and the formula for difference of clovation written as

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + ( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m ) \frac { s ^ { 2 } } { \rho }
$$

The dropped terms or factors all represent quantitios of the fourth order in our oxpansion. The term (1-)s²otis largest of such quantities as a rule, and might be noticeable where $\mathcal { D } _ { 1 }$ and $\boldsymbol { D } _ { 2 }$ would not be.

Probably for short lines and small differencos of elevation tho most convenient formula would be

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = A s \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + A ( { \scriptstyle \frac { 1 } { 2 } } - m ) { \frac { \vartheta ^ { 2 } } { \rho } }
$$

and for other lines formula (7).

(Numbered as in foregoing discussion)

Former Coast and Geodetic Survey form,

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \ \tan { \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) } \biggl [ 1 + \frac { h _ { 1 } + h _ { 2 } } { 2 \ \rho } + \frac { \ s ^ { 2 } } { 1 2 \rho ^ { 2 } } \biggr ] .
$$

Reference: Page 2lo and General Instructions for Field Work Coast and Geodetic Survey，pages 34-37 (edition of 1908).

Logarithmic form,

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \ \mathrm { t a n } \left( { \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } } \right) A B C .
$$

Reference: Page 209 and tables.

Case II. Nonreciprocal observations

Former Coast and Geodetic Survey form,

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + \frac { 0 . 5 - m } { \rho } s ^ { 2 } + \frac { ( 1 - m ) \ s ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } } { \rho } .
$$

Reference: Page 2ll and General Instructions for Field Work Coastand Geodetic Survey，pages 34-37 (edition of 1908).

Corrected form,

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = A D _ { 1 } \ s \ \cot \zeta _ { 1 } + \frac { 0 . 5 - m } { \rho } A D _ { 2 } \ s ^ { 2 } + \frac { ( 1 - m ) \ s ^ { 2 } \cot ^ { 2 } \zeta _ { 1 } } { \rho } \quad .
$$

Reference: Page 213 and tables.

"Exact"form,

$$
\begin{array} { r } { \gamma _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s A R \frac { \cos { \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - \left( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m \right) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } \right] } } { \sin { \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - \left( \frac { 1 } { 2 } - m \right) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } - \frac { s } { 2 \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } \right] } } \quad . } \end{array}
$$

Reference: Page 2ll and tables;also Forimulae and Tables for Position Computation, Coast and Geodetic Survey Special Publication No.8, for $R$

See also additional note, page 220.

# NOTES ON CONSTRUCTION AND USE OF TABLES

The tables are constructed with mean values of $\rho$ and $_ { m }$ log $\rho = 6 . 8 0 4 4 4$ corrosponding to mean radius of curvature in latitude $4 0 ^ { \circ }$ for Clarke's spheroid of 1866.

$\dot { m } = 0 . 0 6$ . m varics between O.05 and 0.10 in the great majority of cases. This value near the smaller limit was taken as probably nearer the truth for tho high lines, in which the correction terms tabulated are most likely to appear, than an intermediate value of 0.07 or 0.08

A， $B$ ， $c$ ， $\mathcal { P } _ { 1 }$ and $D _ { z }$ are all very near unity. To compute their logarithms the approximate expression log $( 1 + x ) = M x$ was used, $\mathcal { M }$ being the modulus of common logarithms =0.43429.

Formulas for constructing tables:

$$
A = 1 + { \frac { h } { \rho } } \qquad \log \ A = { \frac { M h } { \rho } } \qquad h = { \frac { \rho } { \operatorname { i f } } } \log \ A = 1 4 6 . 7 8 \log \ A
$$

$\log A$ being in units of fifth place.

$$
\begin{array} { r l } & { \mathcal { B } - 1 + \frac { \delta } { 2 \rho } \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 3 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \qquad \log \mathcal { B } - \frac { \lambda \delta } { 2 \rho } \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 3 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) } \\ & { \qquad \log \Biggl [ \arctan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 3 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \Biggr ] - \log 2 \frac { \delta } { 2 \rho } + \log ( \log \mathcal { B } ) } \\ & { \qquad - 7 . 4 6 \pi ^ { 7 } \left( 1 + \log ( \log \mathcal { B } ) \right) } \\ & { \mathcal { C } - 1 + \frac { \delta ^ { 2 } } { 1 2 2 \rho ^ { 2 } } \qquad \log \mathcal { C } - \frac { 3 \delta \delta ^ { 2 } } { 1 2 \rho ^ { 2 } } \qquad \log \varepsilon - \log \Biggl ( \rho \sqrt { \frac { 1 2 } { \mathcal { N } } } \Biggr ) + \frac { \gamma \log ( \log \mathcal { C } ) } { 2 } } \\ & { \qquad - 7 . 6 2 \rho \mathrm { i } + \frac { 1 } { \mathcal { N } } \log ( \log \mathcal { C } ) } \\ & { \mathcal { D } _ { i } - 1 + \frac { 6 } { 8 } ( 1 - m ) ^ { 3 } - \frac { \delta ^ { 2 } } { \rho ^ { 2 } } \qquad \log \mathcal { D } _ { i } - \frac { 3 \delta } { 2 \rho ^ { 2 } } \qquad \mathrm { i } \mathrm { e } ^ { \mathcal { B } } ( 1 - m ) ^ { 3 } - \frac { \delta ^ { 2 } } { 2 } } \\ & { \log \varepsilon - 7 . 0 5 7 \sin \cdot 2 \mathrm { i } \log ( \log \mathcal { D } _ { i } ) } \\ & { \mathcal { D } _ { 2 } - 1 + \frac { 1 0 - 2 0 \pi } { 2 4 } \frac { \partial \varepsilon + 5 m ^ { 2 } \delta ^ { 2 } } { \rho ^ { 2 } } \qquad \log \mathcal { D } _ { 2 } - 3 \mathcal { N } \Big ( \frac { \delta } { \mathcal { D } } - 1 0 \mathrm { i } 2 m \Big ) \frac { \delta ^ { 2 } } { \rho ^ { 2 } } } \\ & { \qquad \log \varepsilon - 7 . 0 2 7 \sin ^ { 2 } \mathcal { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \\ & { \qquad \log \varepsilon - 7 . 0 2 7 \sin \mathcal { D } _ { i } ^ { 2 } } \end{array}
$$

The values of $\log A , \log B ,$ etc., wero taken successivelyt0.5,1.5, 2.5,etc.,units of the ffth placeamely,atthe point where thoalue of ,as rounded off to 5 decimals would _change by one $\begin{array} { r } { h , \log \left[ \mathscr { s } \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \right] } \end{array}$ aro carried out far enough so that the values of $\log A , \log B ,$ etc.,may be obtained by interpolation to six decimals. In the numerical examples here given the values of $\log A , \log B ,$ etc.，were computed independently for the actual values of $\rho$ and $\dot { m }$ These results as used in the example allagree within a unit of the sixth decimal place with those found by interpolating in the tables.

The unit of length throughout the tables and formulas is the meter.

# NOTES ON THE DEVELOPMENTS

NoTE 1.-The transformation of (1), page 208,may be conducted rather more simply than is there given.

or

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = \frac { 2 \left( \rho + h _ { 1 } \right) \sin \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \sin \frac { \theta } { 2 } } { \cos \left[ \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) + \frac { \theta } { 2 } \right] }
$$

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = \frac { 2 ~ ( \rho + h _ { 1 } ) \sin \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \sin \frac { \theta } { 2 } } { \cos \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \cos \frac { \theta } { 2 } - \sin \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) \sin \frac { \theta } { 2 } }
$$

Divide numerator and denominator by $\cos \left( { \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } } \right) \cos { \frac { \theta } { 2 } } ,$

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = \frac { 2 \ ( \rho + \tilde { h } _ { 1 } ) \ \tan \displaystyle \frac { \theta } { 2 } \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) } { 1 - \tan \displaystyle \frac { \theta } { 2 } \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) }
$$

or expanding $\tan { \frac { \theta } { 2 } }$ in series and using $\scriptstyle { \theta = { \frac { s } { \rho } } , }$

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } {  { \displaystyle \bar { h } _ { 2 } - \bar { h } _ { 1 } = ( 1 + \frac { \displaystyle \bar { h } } { \displaystyle \rho } ) s ( 1 + \frac { \displaystyle s ^ { 2 } } { \displaystyle 1 2 \rho ^ { 2 } } ) \tan ( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { \displaystyle 2 } ) [ 1 + \frac { \displaystyle s \tan ( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { \displaystyle 2 } ) } { \displaystyle 2 \rho } ] } } \\ & { } & { \quad = s \tan ( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { \displaystyle 2 } ) A B C , . } \end{array}
$$

which is cquation (4).

NoTE 2.--The formula for nonreciprocal observations may be put in the same form as that for reciprocal observations.

From the equation on page 208

$$
\begin{array} { r l r } & { } & { \zeta _ { 2 } = 1 8 0 ^ { \circ } - \zeta _ { 1 } - 2 m \ \theta + \theta \ } \\ & { } & { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } = \ 9 0 - \zeta _ { 1 } + \left( 0 . 5 - m \right) \theta \ ~ } \\ & { } & { \tan \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) = \cot \ [ \zeta _ { 1 } - \left( 0 . 5 - m \right) \theta ] = \cot \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - \left( 0 . 5 - m \right) \frac { s } { \rho \sin { 1 } ^ { \prime \prime } } \right] } \end{array}
$$

Substitute in (4)

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \cot \left[ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 0 . 5 - m ) \frac { s } { \rho \sin 1 ^ { \prime \prime } } \right] A B C
$$

for nonreciprocal observations analogous to

$$
h _ { 2 } - h _ { 1 } = s \tan { \left( \frac { \zeta _ { 2 } - \zeta _ { 1 } } { 2 } \right) } A B C
$$

for reciprocal observations. $B$ should be taken from table with argument

$$
\log s \cot [ \zeta _ { 1 } - ( 0 . 5 - m ) \frac { s } { \rho \sin 1 ^ { \prime \prime } } ] .
$$

This is the present Coast and Geodetic Survey formula for nonreciprocal observations.
